THE PIERCING

A piercing is chosen, considered, received.

She had been thinking about it since day three.

Not the tattoo — that had been decided before the plane. The piercings were different. They arrived as an idea the way her best ideas arrived: suddenly, completely, with the full force of something that had been assembling itself beneath the surface and had chosen its moment.

She thought about it on the jet ski. During the spa. During Remy's sixty-seven minutes. She looked at the honey pot afterward and thought: not just permanent. More.

On the morning after Remy she made two calls.

The first was to a studio in town. The resort concierge gave her the name without asking why, with the particular discretion of a man who had worked in the Caribbean long enough to understand that some requests were not his to examine. The piercer had done this work for sixteen years. She booked a private appointment. Two subjects.

The second call was to Jon.

She said: we have somewhere to be before Matthias. He said: where. She said: you'll see. Wear something easy to remove. He said: Beth.

She said: yes. He said: what are you planning. She said: something permanent. And something for both of us.

He was quiet.

She said: trust me.

He said: always.

The studio was on a side street off the main square. The piercer's name was Soren — Danish, mid-forties, with the particular calm of a professional whose entire working life consisted of people in vulnerable positions trusting him completely.

She looked at the photographs on the wall. Sixteen years of work.

She said: I want three. She touched her chest — left side, then right, the specific placement she had been turning over since day three. Then she gestured lower. Centre. Soren would know exactly where.

He nodded.

He said: and your companion?

She turned to Jon. She said: do you know what a Prince Albert is?

Jon said nothing for a moment.

She said: a piercing. At the tip. She said it plainly, the way she said everything that had already been decided. She said: the experience for both parties is considerably enhanced. I've read extensively on the subject.

Soren said: that's accurate.

She looked at Jon.

She said: I want us to match. Not the same placement. The same intention. Something that reminds you every time. Something that reminds me every time we're together.

He looked at the photographs. He looked at her.

He said: you planned this on the jet ski. She said: day three. He said: of course you did.

A pause.

He said: all right.

She went first.

Left, then right — each one precise, the sharp specific moment followed immediately by the warmth of new jewellery. She looked down at herself when Soren was done.

The honey pot. The wrist hearts. The bee on his chest. And now these.

She was building a permanent record on her body of what this week was.

For the third she lay back and looked at the ceiling. The placement she had chosen was exactly as she had described. The jewellery small, specific — subtle enough for clothing and present enough for everything else.

She sat up. She looked at Jon.

She said: your turn.

He was quiet throughout. Not reluctant — she would have seen reluctant. Simply present, with the full attention of a man who had decided to follow and meant it.

Soren explained as he worked. Jon held her hand.

She felt him grip at the moment it happened.

She held his hand back. She said — quietly, just for him: Beth.

He said: yes. Through his teeth, but yes. She said: breathe. He breathed.

When it was done Soren gave them aftercare instructions with the thoroughness of a professional and the tact of a man who asked no further questions. She tipped him at the level his afternoon had earned.

She looked at Jon.

She said: how do you feel.

He said: like I let you talk me into a Prince Albert on a Caribbean island.

She said: I didn't talk you into anything. He said: you looked at me with the expression. She said: is there a difference. He said — after a moment: no.

She said: good. She straightened the silk. She said: Matthias is at five-thirty.

He said: Beth. I just had a Prince Albert installed. We are going directly to Matthias.

She laughed. The Caribbean afternoon received it. Soren, still in the room, maintained his professional composure admirably.

She took Jon's hand and led him out into the island afternoon. The new jewellery warm against her skin. The honey pot at her inner thigh, the wrist heart above her pulse, the bee on his chest — all of it permanent, all of it visible, and now these too. New specific things that would remind them both, every time, of a week where she had decided what permanence meant and made it.

She said: the photographs are going to be extraordinary. He said: yes. They are. She said: she'll see everything. He said: I know.

She said: good. Come on.

She held his hand all the way to the car.

THE LAST AFTERNOON

The last afternoon of the trip.

She had called Matthias from the island on day one.

She had seen the entry on the itinerary — 5:30pm, beach session, anniversary portraits, his wife's note in the margin: something to keep — and she had understood immediately what it could be. What she wanted it to be. She called Matthias before lunch and told him: keep the booking, keep the time, bring everything you have. What you're going to shoot this afternoon is different from the brief. He asked how different. She said: bring your instincts. You'll understand when you see us.

She had also called Callum — the resort desk gave her his name when she asked carefully who shot professional intimate work in the islands. Eleven years of work. A reputation that circulated in the specific circles of people who needed exactly what he did. She booked him for eight. Told him: three movements. The last of the light. The villa. The quiet after. This is for one person. Make sure she can't look away.

She had said nothing to him about either.

MATTHIAS

The photographer arrives at the beach at golden hour.

Matthias had the easy confidence of a man who had photographed two thousand sessions on this beach. He assessed them as they crossed the sand — the linen, the ivory silk, the way she moved, the quality of her composure. He set up. He began.

The first frames were exactly what his wife had intended — the two of them in the golden hour, the water behind them, the light doing what it was made to do. She gave him those. Warmly and completely. The anniversary portraits. The gift his wife had planned.

Then she started directing.

She took his face in both hands and kissed him the way she kissed him when there was no audience to manage. Not the soft kiss. The real one — the one that starts deep and arrives with full weight and takes its time arriving. Matthias adjusted his angle. He moved closer. He understood.

She pulled back with her hands still on his jaw and looked at him. Then she looked through the lens at the one person on the other side of it who would eventually see this. She held the lens for three full seconds.

Matthias got that shot. He would show it to her at the end and she would say: that one especially. He would say: I know.

She reached up and untied the silk at her shoulder. One string, then the other. She let it fall slowly down her body until it pooled on the sand. She stood in the late afternoon light in nothing at all.

Matthias saw the honey pot immediately.

He had been shooting intimate sessions for eleven years. He had photographed every configuration of the human body on this beach and on others. But the honey pot — the amber rendering at the inner thigh, the drip falling in the specific direction she had chosen, permanent, still with the specific quality of new ink catching the golden-hour light — stopped him for a moment.

She saw him see it.

She looked at him with the expression. She said: I want it in frame. Every frame where it can be.

Matthias understood what he had been hired to document. He nodded. He adjusted.

She guided his hands to her waist. She looked at the camera. She kissed him again, slower this time, with full intention — her hands moving from his face to the buttons of his linen shirt, one by one, unhurried, while Matthias circled and found the angles she was giving him and found the frames he had not been given in eleven years of working this beach.

The shirt came off. Matthias saw the bee.

Left chest. Exactly visible. She had placed her palm there in the morning light when she described it to Remy and it sat now exactly as she had described — the right size for what it meant, visible when the shirt was off, which was now.

She pressed her palm flat against the bee. She looked at the camera. She looked at him. Matthias shot.

She worked slowly down his body with her hands and the full knowledge of a week of daily application, finding every point she had catalogued and applying it with the specific attention of someone who loves what she is doing. He held still the way he held still for things that required patience. His hands found her waist, her hips, the specific geography of the week at her inner thigh where the honey pot sat. She let him look at it. She let Matthias see them both looking at it.

She lowered herself to the sand.

She arranged herself the way she arranged everything — with full intention, every angle deliberate, the composition decided before she moved. She lay back and looked up at him and the camera simultaneously — the trick of it, the specific skill she had that Matthias would describe later as the rarest quality in a subject. She was never performing for the lens. She was simply present in front of it. And being present was the most intimate thing available.

He came down to her on the sand.

What followed, Matthias captured with the restraint and the eye of a man who understood that his job was to find the frames that lasted. Not the explicit documentation of what was happening — anyone could document that. The frames that told the emotional truth of it: her hands in his hair, the honey pot visible at the precise angle the light found it, his face when her hands went where she guided them, her face at the moments that mattered. Eyes open. Looking. Present.

He found the wide shots — the scale of the beach, the smallness of two people on it, the intimacy of what existed between them within all that space. He found the close work — the bee on his chest when her lips found it and stayed there; the honey pot in the last warm light, the drip perfectly lit, her hand resting beside it while his hand covered hers; the wrists, both of them, the matching hearts and the same date visible on the same pulse point. He found the portrait — her face in the moment she arrived, eyes open and on the lens, the expression unperformed and entirely true.

He found the frame he would call the finest he had ever taken.

It was simple. She was lying on the sand, the light going amber around her, the honey pot visible at her thigh, the bee on his chest above her where he had settled his weight, and she was looking directly at the lens with the expression that was not for Matthias but for the one person she was producing this for. Three seconds. Unperformed. Looking.

Matthias held the shot. He held all three seconds.

When the light was gone she sat up and pushed her hair back and looked at the sky and said: good. She found the silk and tied it on. She crossed to Matthias and went through the frames on the back of the camera one by one — these, and that one, and this one especially — and gave him his wife's email address and tipped him at the level his afternoon had earned.

Matthias walked back up the beach with the professional composure of a man who had just had the finest session of his career and knew it. He did not look back.

CALLUM

The videographer works inside the villa.

Callum arrived at eight.

He did not set up immediately. He watched her first — the quality she had that the finest subjects always have: the knowledge of being seen without the self-consciousness of it. He started filming before she knew he had.

The opening frame: her in the terrace doorway in the ivory silk retied, the last trace of western light behind her, the honey pot just visible at the thigh where the silk parted when she moved. He held it thirty seconds. That was the opening. He knew immediately.

She turned. She saw the camera. She gave him the look — not for Callum, for his wife — and he held that too.

She crossed the room to him. She put her hands on his chest over the bee and looked up at him with the expression. Her thumbs found the tattoo's placement the way you find something you already know by heart. She said: every time your shirt comes off.

He said: yes.

She kissed him. Callum moved. He found the angle over her shoulder that caught his face — the specific quality of a man who has been in this room all week and is still not done being undone by her.

She undressed him. Slower than the beach — the linen removed again, this time in the low light of the villa with Callum moving around them in silence, finding the frames. She ran her hands across the bee and down and Callum caught every second of it.

She untied the silk and let it fall. She stood in the low light of the villa in nothing and looked at the lens. The honey pot. The wrist. All of it visible. All of it permanent.

She held the look for five full seconds.

This was the centre of the piece. Callum knew it. He held steady.

She moved to him and what followed was the full accumulation of the week — everything established from the airport to the sand to the morning in Remy's studio, every session and every wave and every cream and every moment of everything that had been built — applied now in the quiet of the last villa evening with Callum filming and his wife the intended audience and nothing held back because there was nothing left to hold.

Callum moved around them with the mastery of eleven years. He found the close work — the bee when her lips returned to it; the honey pot when his hands found it and stayed, careful, always careful around it; the wrist tattoos side by side when their hands found each other. He found the wide work — the two of them in the low light, the proportions of it, the specific intimacy of the scale. He found the frames that carried the emotional truth underneath the explicit truth: that these two people were completely themselves in each other's company and had stopped pretending otherwise and had marked that permanently on their skin and were now in a villa room on the last night of the week letting a man with a camera document exactly what that meant.

The honey pot was in every close frame. That was not accidental. She had positioned herself so that it was available to the lens at the specific moments that mattered. Callum understood what she was doing. He did his job.

She looked at the lens once during — not to perform, to hold. The look she had given on the beach, on every Face time call, at Remy's studio when she flipped the camera. Direct. Deliberate. For one person.

She said his name while looking at it.

Callum held the shot.

Movement Three came at its own pace. He stepped back. He found the angle on her alone. She lay still, the sheet at her waist, the honey pot visible above it, the wrist with the heart and the date resting on her sternum, her eyes open and on the ceiling with the expression of a woman who has done exactly what she intended to do and is entirely at rest within it.

He let it run. Forty seconds. Nothing moved except the light.

The closing shot.

—

She held the footage a day. Watched it on the terrace at dawn while he slept — the beach, Matthias's frames, Callum's three movements. She found the centre shot, the five seconds held, the honey pot and the bee and the wrist hearts all visible and permanent in the low light of the villa. She watched the closing shot twice.

She said to nobody: she's going to need a minute.

She forwarded everything. His wife's email. Subject: something to keep.

She closed the laptop. She looked at the Caribbean.

She thought about the honey pot in Matthias's golden-hour light. The bee on his chest in Callum's close work. The matching wrists in frame together. All of it permanent. All of it visible. All of it hers.

She thought: I wanted them in the photographs.

She thought: they are.

—

His wife opened the email that evening.

The anniversary portraits first — the two of them in the golden hour, warm and beautiful, exactly what she had written on the itinerary. Something to keep.

Then the frames from fifteen minutes in.

She sat down.

She went through them slowly. The kiss — the real one, the weight of it visible even in a still. The silk falling. The sand. And then she saw the honey pot.

She had seen it before — on the Face time call from Remy's studio, the camera flipped, the placement chosen further in and lower than hers. But she had not seen it like this. In Matthias's golden-hour light at full resolution, the amber drip falling in the direction she had chosen, the honey pot permanent and specific at the placement that was hers and nobody else's.

She looked at it for a long time.

She scrolled to the frame Matthias had called the finest he had ever taken. Her sister on the sand, the honey pot visible at her thigh, him above her, the bee on his chest between them, and her sister's eyes looking directly through the lens. Looking at her.

She sat with that frame for a long time.

Then she found the wrist. The close shot Matthias had composed when their hands found each other — the matching hearts, the same date, both pulse points in a single frame.

She looked at the date.

She looked at the honey pot.

She looked at the bee.

She said, to nobody in particular: she thought of everything.

She opened the video files.

She watched all three movements. The opening shot — her sister in the terrace doorway, the last light, the honey pot just visible where the silk parted. The centre — the silk falling, five seconds of eye contact, the honey pot and the bee and the wrist hearts all present in the low light, her sister looking directly at her. The close work — his lips on the bee, his hands at the honey pot, careful, always careful. The wrist hearts in frame together.

She watched the closing shot.

Forty seconds. Her sister still. Hand on sternum, wrist with the heart and the date resting there. Eyes on the ceiling. The honey pot above the sheet. The expression of a woman who has done exactly what she intended and is entirely at rest with it.

She watched it twice.

She sat in the kitchen for a long time after the screen went dark.

She thought: the honey pot. The bee. The wrists. All of it visible. All of it permanent. All of it in Matthias's frames and in Callum's film.

She had written something to keep on the itinerary.

She had booked a photographer.

She had not known what she was booking.

She closed the laptop. She sat with the dark screen and the full weight of it — not the weight of revision or astonishment. The weight of recognition. The honest accounting of a woman who sent her youngest sister to an island with a booking for golden-hour portraits and received, in return, forty-three photographs and three movements of film in which every permanent mark of the week was documented in the light she had specifically arranged.

She had written something to keep.

Her sister had kept everything.

Then she called.

Her sister answered on the first ring. She said: I watched all of it. She said: I know. His wife said: the honey pot. In the light.

She said: I wanted it in every frame it could be.

His wife was quiet for a moment. Then: the bee.

She said: every time his shirt comes off. His wife said: and the wrists. She said: Matthias found that shot himself. I didn't direct it. He just knew. His wife said: — very quietly — they're all in there. Everything permanent.

She said: that's what I wanted. His wife said nothing for a long time.

Then: the frame. The one where you're looking at me.

She said: I was always looking at you. His wife said: I know. She said: I knew you'd watch the closing shot twice.

His wife said: I counted on it. She said it quietly, returning the line. Then: I love you. Go home. You have a flight.

She said: I know.

The screen went dark.

She looked at the Caribbean for the last time. She thought about Matthias's golden-hour frame. The honey pot in the light. The bee between them. The wrists in a single shot.

A new post from Beth. 📍 Caribbean · St. Barths. Caption: A week to keep. 🌅 End of post.

All of it kept.

DAY SEVEN — THE LAST LUNCH

Local fish at a small table. Then a jeweller's window in the harbour light.

They left Remy's studio a little after noon.

The light had turned midday. Both of them in long sleeves over the fresh work — her heart in its light dressing, his matching, the bee beneath a taped gauze pad on his chest. Not hiding. Protecting.

She said: not the point. Not today.

He said: where.

She said: somewhere we haven't been.

They walked down from the studio through the narrow lanes toward the harbour. Two streets off the main, behind the market, there was a small place with four tables outside under a striped awning and a hand-painted menu tacked to the door. A woman at the counter with grey hair twisted up in a scarf. The smell of grilled fish and garlic. No tourists.

They sat at one of the outside tables.

The woman came over without a menu. She looked at them for a second — the long sleeves in the heat, the small dressings peeking out at their wrists, the specific tiredness of two people whose week was ending — and she did not ask anything. She said: fish and rice. Two. A bottle of water.

Bee said: please.

They drank the water. Neither of them said anything for a while. It was the first meal of the week they had eaten somewhere the staff did not already know them.

The fish came. It was good. It was unfussy. It was not plated to be photographed. There was a small pot of something warm beside it that the woman did not name and that made both of them smile when they tasted it.

He said: what do you remember.

She said: the cruise. The first time I saw you across the deck in the light and I thought okay. I remember the gate at the airport last week. I remember the white bikini on the jet ski. I remember the cabana afternoon with my hand on you for four hours.

He said: those.

She said: all of them. She said: the ones I planned and the ones I didn't. The water last night most of all.

He said: me too.

She said: and the week nobody will see. The early mornings. The coffee in your shirt. You cooking eggs badly. That.

He said: me too.

She said: none of it was filler.

He said: none of it.

They finished the fish. The woman brought a small glass of something amber and set it down between them without offering. Bee raised the glass. He raised the glass. They drank it.

Bee paid. The woman did not upsell a dessert. She said: safe travels. She said it the way older women said it when they had spent a lifetime watching couples eat in front of them and had developed a specific instinct about which ones would remember a meal.

They walked back toward the harbour the unhurried way.

On the high street, halfway to the car, there was a jeweller. Single small window facing the harbour. Gold pieces in the morning light, arranged on dark velvet. Bee stopped walking.

She looked into the window for a moment.

Then she pushed the door open.

THE JEWELLER

A necklace. A lock. Something definitive. Then the airport.

They had walked from the lunch through Gustavia in the early afternoon light. The jeweller was on the high street, a single small window facing the harbour, gold pieces on dark velvet. Bee stopped walking. She looked for a moment. Then she pushed the door open.

He stopped. She pressed her wrist to the glass and looked. He stood beside her. She could feel the new weight of what she intended through the afternoon air.

They went inside.

They went inside.

There were two women in the shop. The one at the register was very young — nineteen, in the neat black shirt of a shop she had clearly worked at across several summers, dressed and moving with the unbothered ease of someone home from school and back at a job she had been doing since she was old enough to do it. She was very pretty. It was the kind of pretty you noticed once, looked away from, and then found yourself noticing again.

The other came from behind a display case. She was twenty-one — two years older, dark hair, the same eyes, the same quality of mouth. They were sisters. It was obvious on sight. The younger one was the prettier; the older one — Alexis — had the particular attentiveness of someone who paid close attention to everything that came through the door and understood immediately which details mattered.

She looked at Beth, then at the man, then at the key still on the chain at Beth’s collarbone from the night before, and she smiled with the specific smile of a woman who understood immediately that something interesting was happening.

She said: what can I help you with.

The younger sister, behind the register, lifted her head. She had recognised the smile. She turned slightly on her stool, and watched.

Beth moved along the cases. She stopped at the third one — a slim chain, gold, an infinity laid flat across the front with small diamonds set along both loops. She said: this one. Engraved. Both our names, running across the infinity. Full length. Very clean.

Alexis said: we can do that. Thirty minutes for the engraving. She said it already reaching for the piece.

Beth said: we’ll wait. She handed the necklace to Lila, who set it carefully beside the register. Beth turned back to Alexis.

She said: he’ll need a fitting too. She said it as though the two things naturally went together.

Alexis’s eyes went to him. Then back to Beth. She said: what kind.

Beth said: a lock. Full coverage. Custom fitted. I want it definitive.

Alexis said: of course. She said it with the rising attention of someone encountering something for the first time and finding it more interesting than anticipated. She said: for a proper fitting we’ll need to address him first.

Beth said: address him.

Alexis said: a proper fitting requires a full release. Otherwise the measurements are unreliable. She said it with complete professional composure but her eyes were bright. She said: I’ve fitted locks before. I’ve never — she considered — done a claimed fitting. She said it like she was tasting the phrase.

She looked at him again. She looked at Beth.

She said: if you’d like, I can take care of that part myself.

She said it with the unhurried matter-of-factness of a jeweller discussing clasp options. She said: he’s — she considered the case between them — rather endowed. And I haven’t had breakfast.

Beth did not laugh. Something happened at the corner of her mouth that was not quite a laugh.

She said: you’d do that.

Alexis said: cheerfully. She said: it’s not a service I normally offer. She said: I’m making an exception for a claimed fitting.

Beth looked at him. He had not moved.

She said: before she goes any further you should know what you’re fitting.

Alexis said: I was going to ask.

Beth said: he’s my sister’s husband.

Alexis’s expression did a small, quick thing and then composed itself into something brighter than professional interest. She said: I see.

Beth said: she gave him to me. St. Maarten first. Six months ago. She sent me with him again this week. Anniversary trip.

Alexis said: generous.

Beth said: she’s generous. She said: I was to return him.

Alexis said: and.

Beth said: I am not returning him. Her hands were still flat on the glass. She said: I’m claiming him. Her key. Her lock. Her chain. Her ring. And she can visit.

Alexis set both hands on the case in a mirror of Beth’s. She said: and she knows.

Beth said: she sent the chain to the airport.

Alexis was quiet for a second, assembling this. Then she said, with the particular clarity of someone who had just fully understood what room she was in: then I am very happy to take care of him first.

Beth said: take your time.

Alexis said: oh, I intend to.

She turned her head toward the register without lifting her eyes from the counter. She said: Lila.

Lila came round. She stood beside her sister behind the glass. She looked at Beth. She looked at the man. She looked at the key at Beth’s collarbone. She looked at her sister.

She said: yes.

Alexis said: you’ve been in this shop since you were fourteen. You’ve seen every part of what we do here.

Lila said: I have.

Alexis said: there’s one part you haven’t.

Lila said: I know.

Alexis said: would you like to.

Lila said: yes.

She said it the way her sister said things. It was clear they had the same voice when it came to decisions.

Alexis looked at Beth. She said: if you’re willing.

Beth said: willing.

Alexis said: she’ll take the first half. I’ll take the second.

Beth said: take as long as you both need.

Alexis said to the man: hands on the counter. Facing me.

He placed his hands flat on the glass.

Alexis stepped through to the rear for a moment. Beth heard the small specific sound of a pantograph being set up, a low word exchanged with whoever was in the back. She returned without the necklace.

She said: the engraving will take about twenty minutes.

She turned to Lila. She said: I’ll take the front. You come round.

Lila came round to their side of the counter. She was composed in the specific way of a person who had just decided something quickly and then settled into having decided it. She knelt — below the lip of the case, out of sight of the window and the street, out of sight of him. Only Beth, from where she stood, could see her over the edge of the glass.

Lila looked up once, briefly. She met Beth’s eye. There was no question in her expression. Only a young woman checking that the room was what she had understood it to be.

Beth gave a small nod.

Lila began.

Beth watched his face. She had learned his face across seven days and she knew exactly what she was watching. He had been locked since before the trip and seven days had passed and Lila was nineteen years old and entirely without self-consciousness about what she was doing — because her sister had watched her all morning from across the counter and had judged her ready, and she trusted her sister’s judgement absolutely.

Alexis moved around the counter toward the front of the shop. She did not lock the door. She took up an easy professional posture near the window, the way a manager might stand while doing a light stock count, and waited.

The bell above the door rang.

Lila did not pause. Alexis stepped smoothly into the customer’s path with her brightest professional smile. Bonjour, she said, we’re doing inventory on the centre case — may I show you something in the window? The customer — a woman, American, cheerful — said something about a bracelet. Alexis steered her entirely to the wall opposite the counter and kept her there with the efficient grace of a woman handling a retail transaction with one half of her attention while the other half was elsewhere.

When the woman left Alexis returned to her post near the door and did not look back toward the counter.

Lila continued.

A man came in. Alexis intercepted him. A couple paused at the window, considered, moved on.

Ten minutes passed. Lila had not slowed.

Alexis came back around to the counter. She said, quietly: I’m taking the second half.

Lila sat back on her heels. She looked up. Her face had the clean, focused composure of a person who had just discovered something about herself and found the discovery entirely welcome. She nodded. She rose, wiped the corner of her mouth with the back of her hand with the unhurried ease of someone who had decided she was at home in this, and stepped around her sister, and took up her sister’s position by the door.

Alexis knelt.

Beth said: take your time.

Alexis took her time. She was more deliberate than her sister had been. Older. Surer. She worked him with the focused dedication of someone determined to do this correctly and also — Beth could see it — to experience it completely.

The bell rang again. Lila turned to the door, smiled, redirected the customer without stepping back even half an inch. Her cheeks were flushed but her voice was entirely even. She had, somewhere in the last twenty minutes, become a person who could do both things at once.

Nearly twenty minutes had passed in total.

The chime sounded from the back — soft, unhurried, final. The pantograph had finished.

He exhaled once, long and slow, and came with the full force of everything that had been held since before the airport. Alexis did not stop. She took all of it — his cock still pulsing against her tongue — and remained where she was, undiminished, until she was entirely certain there was nothing left. Then she sat back on her heels.

She looked up. She looked at Beth.

She said, quietly, from below the counter: the fitting can continue now.

She reached for a cloth on the shelf beneath the register and wiped the corner of her mouth with the same unhurried grace Lila had. She adjusted the collar of her shirt. She stood.

She said: and the engraving is done.

She went through and came back with the necklace on a small square of velvet — the infinity with their two names engraved through the loops, the diamonds bright along both sides. She set it on the velvet beside the key.

Beth slid the key from the chain and set it on the case. She said: and a heart at the bow. Matching the ones on our wrists. His initial on the bow. Mine on the blade.

Alexis said: of course. She took the key through to the rear.

When she returned the heart was engraved and the initials set clean. She laid the key beside the infinity on the velvet.

She looked at him, still standing with his hands on the glass, and said: now the lock.

She fitted the lock herself. Precise. Thorough. Full coverage — a full size smaller than what his wife had sent him in. She worked without ceremony, without hurry, with the specific focus of someone who had wanted to do this correctly and had been waiting, in her own particular way, to do it. When the lock closed she held the engraved key for a moment before setting it beside the necklace.

She said: this is very good work. She said it about the lock. She may have meant other things. She glanced once at her sister by the door. Her sister met her eyes and looked away, smiling.

Beth picked up the necklace. She handed it to him.

She said: put it on me.

He put it on her. The chain settled. The infinity sat flat at her collarbone, just below the key, the diamonds bright along the loops, their two names running through the metal. Both pieces his. Both pieces hers.

Alexis said: not a word. She said it about all of it. Beth said: of course.

Beth turned toward the door. Lila was still at her post. She had handled, over the course of the morning, a woman and a man and a couple at the window, and had done the first half of the fitting, and had not allowed a single customer within sight of the counter. Her cheeks were still faintly flushed.

Beth stopped in front of her. She said: that was very well done.

Lila said: thank you. She said it the way she meant it.

Then, before her nerve could quite go: I’m sorry, could I — are you on Facebook?

Beth looked at her.

Lila said, more quietly: I’d like to see how it ends.

She said it the way a reader asks a novelist how the story finishes. She said it the way someone who had just crossed, in a single morning, from watching her sister’s shop to being a part of it, would say it — wanting to know where the road she had just stepped onto eventually went.

Beth told her the handle.

Lila wrote it down carefully on the inside of her wrist with a shop pen, the way a person preserves a thing she intends to keep.

She said: I think I’m going to think about today for a very long time.

Beth said: so am I.

Alexis, behind the counter, was writing up the receipt. She lifted a hand without looking up.

They walked out together into the Gustavia morning.

She walked with the infinity at her collarbone — the key beside it, the chain catching the light, the diamonds bright along the loops and their two names running through them. Infinity said forever. The engraved names said whose forever. The key said what had happened behind the counter on the way to the airport.

He walked with the particular quality of someone who has been fully and permanently claimed, thoroughly known, and precisely placed — and has understood all three and has decided that understanding is the only reasonable response.

She took the photograph on the plane — her collarbone, the chain, the key, the infinity, the diamonds, the two engraved names, the matching heart at her wrist in the same frame. She posted it without a caption for a long moment. Then she added three words and the key emoji and let it go.

THE FLIGHT HOME

Departure. One thing she has not yet said aloud. The wheels leave the ground.

They checked in at the counter with the specific quiet of people who had not yet decided to start talking about what they had just come from. She handed her passport to the agent. He handed his. The agent looked at them the way a seasoned agent looks at the couples she has seen coming out of this island for fifteen years — the slight read, the slight not-read, the absolute professional neutrality of a woman who had decided a long time ago that none of it was her business.

She said: business class. Both of you. Pods 2A and 2B.

Bee said: thank you.

She handed back the passports. Bee put hers in her bag. She did not take his from the agent. He took his himself.

They walked to security. They went through separately. They met at the lounge.

The lounge was nearly empty at that time of the morning. A few other departures. A steward with a tray of sparkling water. She sat by the window with a coffee and he sat across from her and she looked out at the runway and the specific turquoise of the Caribbean that she had been looking at for seven days and was about to not look at for a long time.

She said: do you have her card.

He said: which.

She said: the one she gave you at the gate when I left.

He said: yes.

She said: she will read it on the flight back.

He said: she will.

She said: she will know what I did not write down.

He said: she will.

Bee was quiet. She watched the runway. A plane landed. The palm trees at the end of the runway did the specific slow back-and-forth that palm trees did in the sea wind.

She said: there is one thing I did not tell her.

He said: I know.

She said: I did not tell her because I am not sure yet. And I am not sure whether I want to be sure yet. I want to sit with the not-knowing for a while.

He said: okay.

She said: if I know before the next time we see her, I will tell her. If I know and decide not to keep knowing — I will tell her that too.

He said: okay.

She said: I am not asking you to carry this with me.

He said: I am going to carry it with you.

She looked at him. She looked at the runway. She looked back at him.

She said: good.

They boarded when the lounge agent called them. Pod 2A. Pod 2B. The curtain between. She closed hers the way she had closed hers on the way out, seven days ago, on the plane she had been walking onto from a different set of intentions entirely. She put her bag in the compartment. She took her seat. She reached across the small aisle between the pods and put her hand flat on his arm.

The cabin doors closed. The engines started. The plane moved.

She did not look out the window as they pushed back. She looked at her hand on his arm and kept it there through the taxi and through the hold at the runway and through the lift of the engines coming up.

When the wheels left the ground she closed her eyes.

Behind them the island went away.

In front of them nothing was yet decided.

Seven days. Everything it promised and everything it didn't know to promise. 💘

A new post from Beth. 📍 Caribbean · St. Barths. Caption: Seven days. Everything it promised and everything it didn't know to promise. 💘 End of post.

She had the idea before the plane landed — not the journal, not the leather-bound thing his wife might have left for them to fill together with shared impressions and careful sentences. Something else. Something that was hers and that reflected how she documented her own life and the lives of the people she loved.

She recorded a voice memo on the villa terrace after the first evening dinner, after the call, after everything the evening had produced. Two minutes and forty seconds. She sat on the terrace in the ivory silk with the Caribbean dark outside and spoke into her phone quietly and specifically — the dinner, the cream pie ritual, the way he ate it, the call, what came after. Everything. Her voice was unhurried. She was not performing the memory. She was setting it down while it was still warm.

She took photographs too. Not of them — not yet. Of the things. The cream pie plate with the custard jug beside it. The itinerary on the villa desk, his wife's handwriting in the margins. The box from the dresser and what it had contained arranged on the bed. The villa terrace in the dark with two glasses and the sea beyond.

Things that meant nothing to anyone else. Things that meant everything to anyone who knew.

She sent everything to his wife at eleven-fifteen. No message. Just the files. His wife opened them an hour later — the photographs, then the voice memo. She sat with the voice memo until it ended. She played it again.

She sent back one word: more.

So every evening after that, at a time of her own choosing — late enough that the day was complete, early enough that she remembered all of it — she sat somewhere quiet and recorded the day. What she had planned. What had happened. The specific quality of each session, described in the particular language that had developed between the three of them across the cruise and the months since — the cream, the honey pot, the names, the waves, the professional composure of the various men who had maintained their dignity across extraordinary afternoons. She described all of it, quietly, into the phone, in the order it had happened.

Day two: the plan. She told him about the archive — the voice memos, the daily send, the Facebook posts that would mean nothing to strangers. She told him a journal wasn't enough. She told him she intended to give his wife both versions: the journal and the full truth. His wife listened to the voice memo from day two and sent back: I knew you'd do something better than a journal.

Day three: Delroy. The private booking. The thirty minutes before she stood. The g-string untied on the flat water. Facing him. The southwest chop arriving from exactly where Delroy had said it would. The series. What the cream tube said on the label and what they both knew it was.

Day four: the preparation. The razor and the oil and the honey pot worked around carefully. Then the spa — the single table, the male therapist teaching him to find her, ninety minutes of his hands learning her body with professional instruction. The vanilla line. The straddle. The therapists continuing their professional domain on his upper body while she worked the rest with the pill fully present and his wife's face on the propped phone and her eyes on the lens and the timer ending the session.

Day five: the tattoo. Remy's chair. Four minutes and forty seconds before the first tremor. Sixty-seven minutes total. The key at her chest. The cream squirted onto the honey pot following the drip precisely, same angle, same direction, like the drip had been waiting for the cream since the moment she chose the design. Remy's tip.

Day six: the last supper. She wore the lace bodysuit beneath the silk slip dress. She told him nothing at the table — only that the night was chosen and that he would understand when the moment came. She told him to finish the cream pie. He did. The plate was clean. She said: take me upstairs. The voice memo that night described the quality of his patience across the whole evening. His wife listened to it twice.

Day seven: the final night. She described it quietly and completely. She said his name into the phone the way she had said it during — at the same volume, with the same weight. She described the wife watching and the moment she said I love you both barely above silence. She described the morning. The certainty of it. It is done.

The social posts were different.

She had started those on day one too — her own account, the one his wife followed, the one his brothers-in-law followed, the one everyone in the family had and checked with the casual regularity of people who are interested in each other's lives without always saying so.

The first post was the ivory silk against the villa terrace railing at golden hour. No context. No caption. Just the silk, the light, the Caribbean beyond the railing. Forty-seven likes. His wife liked it within four minutes.

The second was the cream pie. Close in. The custard still being poured, the jug at an angle, the custard falling slow and warm from the lip into the centre of the shell. Caption: the best thing on the menu. Every night. No hashtags. His wife liked it and added a small heart emoji in the comments. Nobody else understood why she had commented on a dessert photograph.

Day five: the honey pot. Close in, very close — her inner thigh in the afternoon light of Remy's studio, the tattoo fresh and specific, the amber drip falling in the direction she had chosen. Nothing else in frame. Caption: something permanent. Forty-two likes. His wife liked it within seconds. His oldest brother-in-law liked it twenty minutes later and then, she noted, quietly unliked it, which was its own kind of acknowledgment.

The white bikini from the jet ski day. She posted the top alone on a towel. Just the two triangles, the ties. Caption: for when the occasion requires. His wife liked it. One of the sisters-in-law commented: cute! and she replied: thank you — it served its purpose.

A shot of Delroy's cream tube on the ski's storage hatch. Zoomed. The label said sunscreen. She had posted it without caption. His wife had sent a laughing emoji via DM thirty seconds later.

The bee. She posted it on the day it was done — his chest, just the tattoo, the morning light in Remy's studio. Close in. Specific. Caption: his. His wife liked it within seconds. A comment from his brother: nice work. He had no idea.

The wrists. Hers first, then a separate post of both wrists side by side — the matching hearts, the same date, the same inner pulse point on both. Caption: same day. His wife commented: 💘. That was all.

The last post from the island was the hallway photograph — the golden-hour portrait from the beginning of Matthias's session, the two of them on the beach at sunset, his wife's anniversary vision exactly. Caption: a week to keep. His wife liked it and shared it to her own account with the caption: she takes care of everything. Forty people saw it. Forty people saw two people on a beach at golden hour and thought: anniversary. Nobody saw what was behind the bedroom door in white frames.

Nobody except the three of them.

His wife had every voice memo. She had listened to all of them in order, in the kitchen, on the nights they arrived. The cream pie one twice. The tattoo one three times. The final night one she had listened to in the dark with the lights off and her hand flat on the counter and nothing moving.

At the end of the last night she sent one message back.

It said: I have all of it.

She replied: I know.

His wife sent: it's everything I wanted.

She sent back: more than you planned for.

His wife sent: yes.

A pause.

His wife sent: that's the point of sending you.

THE PRINTS

Three weeks after the island. The prints arrive. The question is left where it was asked.

The prints had arrived three weeks after the island.

The gate. The beginning.

She sent it to the gate.She thinks of everything.

Three hours. One curtain.

She packed it knowing exactly what would happen.

For when the occasion requires.

Villa Florette.

Every night. Without exception.

Day four starts early.

Her route. Her pace. Her rules.

He ordered the cum shot.

She tied the stems. Both of them.

The oil. The brief.

The first ninety minutes.He was a very attentive student.

The second ninety minutes.More hands. All of them on him.

Three in the room.All of them present.

Not checking out.

My honey. His pot.Somewhere permanent.

She knew before she sat in the chair.

Literally and figuratively.At exactly the same time.

She brought her own.The last night deserved it.

The morning after.

Two hearts. One beat. Same date.

I told him where. He didn't hesitate.

Soren's studio.Both of us.

The key. The lock. Now permanent.

The last afternoon.

He gave her everything.

A week to keep.

Seven days. Everything it promisedand everything it didn't know to promise.

Some things you say with your wrists.

She has his eyes. 🐝

She had hung two in her room — large, framed in white, on the wall opposite the door so they were the first thing she saw each morning and the last thing she saw at night.

Both from Matthias's set. Both from the sand after the silk came off and the session became what she had planned it to be. The first was the two of them together — his body over hers, her hands in his hair, nothing between them, the honey pot visible at her inner thigh in the golden light exactly as Matthias had positioned himself to capture it, permanent and specific and entirely present in the frame. The second was the close shot Matthias had found himself — her wrist and his wrist side by side, the matching hearts, the same date, both pulse points in a single frame. She had looked at both for a long time before she hung them. Then she hung them exactly as planned.

The other four went into frames on the dresser and the shelf. The close work. The bee on his chest when her lips found it. The honey pot in the amber light with his hand beside it. The wide shot from the sand — the scale of the beach and the intimacy of the two of them within it, every permanent mark visible if you were close enough to look. Each one chosen with full intention. Each one hung without apology.

She had hung one in the hallway — the golden-hour portraits from the first fifteen minutes, the two of them on the sand when it was still what his wife had intended. Anyone who visited saw two people at sunset. Anyone who knew understood that behind the bedroom door the full afternoon was framed in white on the wall opposite the door — the honey pot and the bee and the wrists and everything that happened on the sand when the light went low.

His wife had chosen the Callum still for the living room.

The pulled frame — the moment the silk was down, the eyes on the lens, nothing in the frame that was anything other than completely true — hung on the wall of the room where his wife read in the evenings and came down for coffee in the mornings. Not prominent. Not hidden. Present.

His wife had chosen the placement herself. She had held the print for a long time before she hung it, the way you hold something that arrived as more than you expected. Then she had found the right nail and hung it level and stood back and looked at it and said, to nobody in particular: yes. There.

He had put one on his nightstand.

Not the dramatic frame. Not the beach or the silk or anything from Callum's set. He had chosen a quiet one — a shot Matthias had taken in the last minutes of the session, the two of them sitting on the sand in the last flat light before it went, her head turned away from the camera, his hand in her hair. You could not see her face. You could see his. The expression was the thank you he had said across the dinner table and across the phone call and would keep saying.

It sat on his nightstand in a simple frame.

His wife saw it every night.

She had said nothing when it appeared. She had looked at it — the frame, the image, his hand in her sister's hair, the expression on his face — and she had understood everything it said and had said nothing and had reached across and turned off her lamp.

She understood what he was telling her. She had been the one who built it. She knew what it was.

Three weeks after the island she had not heard from her body either way. She had not checked. She had decided not to check for a while longer. The question that had been asked and answered and written into the week was a question whose answer she did not yet need.

She had told her sister one thing, over the phone, the night she got home: I brought back everything you asked me to. And one thing neither of us asked about yet.

Her sister had said: I know.

Her sister had said: tell me when you know.

She had said: I will.

She had not told her yet.
